### Question 1: I am writing to request your review of my course tracking in preparation for graduation. I am pursuing a double major in Computer Science and Applied Mathematics. I have attached the spreadsheet tracking my courses for your review. I would appreciate it if you could take a look and provide any necessary feedback: Humility, Global Humanities and Social Change, Linear Algebra, Modern Vietnamese Culture and Society, Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age, Discrete Math, Computer Science II: Data Structures, Programming for Data Science and Visualization, Design and Systems Thinking, Scientific Inquiry, Computer Organization, Calculus, Intelligent Robot Studio: From Theory to Practice, How to become a writer: lessons from Vietnamese and non-Vietnamese writers, Introduction to Political Science, The Global Vietnam War II, Deep Learning for Artificial Intelligence, Advanced Deep Learning, Probability, Computer Science I: Introduction to Programming, Algorithms and Theory of Computing, Database System, Advanced Programming Techniques, Introduction to Artificial Intelligence, Machine Learning for Data Science, Multivariable Calculus, Mathematical Statistics, General Work Program (GWP) 1, [Independent Research Project] Labeled FreSCo- Mining Frequent Patterns in labeled Simplicial Complexes, Object-Oriented Analysis and Design, Statistical Learning, Operating Systems, Abstract Algebra, Differential Equations, Stochastic Calculus, Natural Language Processing, Advanced Linear Algebra.  
### Answer:

Additional Courses:
- Discrete Math (Completed)
- Computer Organization (Completed)
- Intelligent Robot Studio: From Theory to Practice (Completed)

- Introduction to Political Science (Irrelevant to the major)
- The Global Vietnam War II (Irrelevant to the major)
- General Work Program (GWP) 1 (Irrelevant to the major)
- [Independent Research Project] Labeled FreSCo- Mining Frequent Patterns in labeled Simplicial Complexes (Irrelevant to the major)

It appears that you have completed most of the required courses for both majors. However, there are a few points to consider:

1. Ensure that all elective and experiential learning requirements are met. This typically includes electives in Computer Science and Applied Mathematics, as well as any experiential learning components such as internships or capstone projects.

2. Verify that you have met the total credit requirement of 128 credits. Double-check that all courses have been correctly counted and that you have not missed any required courses.

3. Review the specific requirements for the Independent Research Project to ensure it aligns with the expectations for both majors.

4. Confirm that you have completed any additional requirements such as a capstone course or senior project, if applicable.

5. Since you are on a double major track, ensure that you have not taken any overlapping courses that could be counted only once towards your degree requirements.

6. Check the academic calendar for any deadlines related to graduation application and ensure all paperwork is submitted on time.

7. If you are on academic probation, make sure you have met the probationary requirements for good academic standing in your final term, even if you are graduating.

Please note that this review is based on the information provided and assumes that all course descriptions and requirements are accurately represented. If there are any discrepancies or additional requirements, you should consult with your academic advisor for clarification.

Lastly, ensure that you have a clear understanding of the graduation application process and any specific steps required by Fulbright University Vietnam. Good luck with your graduation!

### Question 2: Can you differentiate a cross-listed course and elective applied course in the case of a double major?  
### Answer: 

Cross-listed courses are those that are shared between two different majors (or a major and a minor) and can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements up to a limit of 8 credits (two courses) per pair. This means that if majors A and B share a cross-listed course, a student can apply up to 8 credits from that course toward both their A and B major requirements.

On the other hand, elective applied courses are courses that a student chooses to take to fulfill their elective requirements within their major(s) or minor(s). These courses are not necessarily shared between different disciplines but are selected by the student to complement their studies and may not have a specific inter-major agreement for double-counting.

In the context of a double major, cross-listed courses allow students to streamline their curriculum by using the same course to fulfill requirements for both majors, up to the 8-credit limit. Elective applied courses, however, are chosen individually by the student to meet the specific needs of their academic program and do not have a shared credit limit between majors.

For example, if a student is majoring in both Biology (major A) and Psychology (major B), and there is a cross-listed course "Human Behavior and Physiology" that is offered by both departments, the student could use this course to fulfill 8 credits for both Biology and Psychology. However, if the student chooses to take an elective course in "Environmental Science" for their Biology major and another in "Cognitive Psychology" for their Psychology major, these courses would not be cross-listed and would not contribute to the 8-credit cross-listed course limit. Instead, they would be applied as electives within their respective majors.

In summary, cross-listed courses are shared between majors and can be double-counted up to a limit, while elective applied courses are chosen individually by the student to meet specific major requirements and do not have a shared credit limit between majors.

### Question 3: I would like to ask for the capstone withdrawal policy. What will be the impact when I choose to drop the capstone before the Fall 2025 term starts? Will there be any penalties associated with withdrawing, such as a 'W' notation on my transcript?  
### Answer: 

The capstone project is likely a requirement for graduation, so withdrawing from it could have significant implications for your academic progress and graduation timeline. However, based on the provided information, the policy for withdrawing from a course generally applies.

For regular courses, there are two withdrawal periods per term:

1. The first period starts after 4:00 PM Friday, Week 2 to 4:00 PM Friday, Week 4. During this time, students can withdraw from a course without any consequence, and a "W" grade will not appear on their transcript.

2. The second period starts after 4:00 PM Friday, Week 4 to 4:00 PM Friday, Week 8. During this period, attempted credits and a "W" grade will be recorded on the transcript, but this grade does not affect a student’s GPA.

If you withdraw from the capstone project during the first withdrawal period, you should not receive a "W" grade on your transcript. However, if you withdraw during the second withdrawal period, a "W" grade will be recorded.

If you withdraw after the halfway point of the capstone project (end of week 4 for an 8-week course or end of week 8 for a full-term course), you will automatically receive an 'F' grade, which will affect your GPA.

Regarding the capstone specifically, the policy might be slightly different due to its unique nature as a culminating project. It's important to consult with your primary advisor and the Registrar's Office to understand the specific capstone withdrawal policy, as it may have additional considerations or requirements.

For example, the provided information mentions an appeal process for disputing the final grade of the capstone project, which suggests that there might be specific rules and procedures for withdrawing from or completing the capstone successfully.

In terms of penalties, withdrawing from the capstone could mean not completing the requirement, which could delay your graduation. Additionally, if you withdraw after the halfway point, you will receive an 'F' grade, which will negatively impact your GPA.

To avoid any penalties, it is crucial to:

- Consult with your primary advisor and the Registrar's Office as soon as possible.
- Understand the specific deadlines and policies for capstone project withdrawals.
- Consider the implications of withdrawing on your academic progress and graduation timeline.

Remember that the provided information does not cover all possible scenarios, and the actual policy might differ slightly. Always seek direct advice from the relevant academic and administrative offices at your institution.

### Question 4: Questions related to course history, in which one course can have multiple IDs? Like Advanced Deep Learning course. How would it be handled among different cohorts?  
### Answer: 

Course history would be tracked using a unique identifier, such as a course ID, even if the course has multiple IDs across different cohorts. This is important for maintaining a clear and consistent record of a course's offerings, progress, and outcomes over time.

For instance, the "Advanced Deep Learning" course might have different IDs for each cohort it is offered to, such as "DL101-2023" for the 2023 cohort and "DL102-2024" for the 2024 cohort. However, the course's history would be linked to a master course ID that remains constant, regardless of the cohort-specific ID.

In a system designed to handle this, each cohort's instance of the course would be associated with the master course ID, allowing for the tracking of:

1. **Course Content Evolution**: Changes in curriculum, instructors, and learning materials over time.
2. **Student Progress**: Tracking individual student progress across different cohorts.
3. **Assessment and Outcomes**: Analyzing assessment results and outcomes for each cohort against the overall course standards.
4. **Instructor and TA Assignments**: Keeping a record of who taught the course in each cohort.
5. **Resources and Materials**: Managing access to course resources and materials that might be updated or revised over time.

This approach ensures that while each cohort might have a unique identifier for administrative purposes, the core course remains identifiable and its history intact. This is crucial for educational institutions and platforms that offer courses repeatedly with updates or revisions, ensuring continuity and consistency in course delivery and management.

### Question 5: I have taken Microeconomic Analysis and Macroeconomic Analysis in Fall 2023. Am I eligible to take Financial Economics in Spring 2024?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you are eligible to take Financial Economics in Spring 2024. According to the given context, students must complete at least one of Microeconomic Analysis and Macroeconomic Analysis before taking Advanced level courses, which include Financial Economics. Since you have completed both Microeconomic Analysis and Macroeconomic Analysis in Fall 2023, you meet the prerequisite requirement to enroll in Financial Economics in the Spring 2024 semester.

### Question 6: If I have waived the course Computer Science I, but I want to declare Computer Science major, do I have to take Computer Science I?  
### Answer: 

No, you do not have to take Computer Science I if you have waived the course. To declare a Computer Science major, you must complete at least two Computer Science courses, and MATH 202 (Discrete Math) is also counted towards this requirement. Therefore, you can substitute the waived course with another Computer Science course to meet the major requirements. However, it is important to check with your academic advisor to ensure that your course selection aligns with the major's curriculum and that you are meeting all necessary requirements.

### Question 7: If I have taken the following courses: Mathematical Statistics, Abstract Algebra, Linear Algebra, Multivariable Calculus, Calculus, Differential Equation, Stochastic Calculus, Statistical Learning, Discrete Mathematics, Probability, am I eligible to declare a minor in Applied Mathematics? What about majoring in Applied Mathematics?  
### Answer: 

To declare a minor in Applied Mathematics, you would typically need to complete at least 6 Mathematics courses, which include at least 1 Advanced course and 2 Intermediate courses. Based on the courses you've listed, you have:

* Foundation Courses: Linear Algebra, Multivariable Calculus, Calculus (which might be considered as part of the Single Variable Calculus foundation course)
* Intermediate Courses: Differential Equations, Probability, Discrete Mathematics
* Advanced Courses: Mathematical Statistics, Stochastic Calculus, Statistical Learning

Given this, you have:

* 1 Pure Mathematics course (Abstract Algebra)
* 4 Intermediate courses (Differential Equations, Probability, Discrete Mathematics, possibly Multivariable Calculus if it's considered an intermediate level course)
* 3 Advanced courses (Mathematical Statistics, Stochastic Calculus, Statistical Learning)

Since you have more than the required number of Intermediate courses (2 are needed for the minor), and you have met the Advanced course requirement (1 is needed for the minor), you would likely be eligible to declare a minor in Applied Mathematics. 

For majoring in Applied Mathematics, the requirements are more extensive and typically include:

* Completion of at least 2 Foundation courses or 1 Foundation and 1 Intermediate course
* Graduation with Honors Requirements (Capstone I and Capstone II with Honors)
* Advanced coursework in specific applied areas (e.g., Applied Statistics, Engineering Mathematics, Data Science)

Given your course list, you have fulfilled the Foundation course requirement (assuming Calculus is considered part of the foundation), but you would still need to:

* Complete additional Applied Mathematics courses to meet the major's requirements, especially in the Advanced level (e.g., more courses in Applied Statistics, Engineering Mathematics, Data Science)
* Consult with an academic advisor to ensure proper course selection and to plan out the remaining coursework needed for the major

In summary, you are eligible to declare a minor in Applied Mathematics based on your current course list. For majoring in Applied Mathematics, you would need to complete more coursework and possibly additional electives in specific applied areas, and you should consult with an academic advisor to ensure you meet all the major requirements.

### Answer:

You have completed the necessary 100-level major requirements, which include Introduction to Art History and Theory and Introduction to Film History and Theory. According to the eligibility criteria, all 100-level major requirements must be complete by the beginning of Capstone I, and you have met this requirement. Therefore, you are eligible to apply for the capstone provided you also meet the other criteria such as maintaining a major GPA of 3.6 and having no outstanding Incompletes at the time of application. It is also important to note that you should consult early with your prospective advisor to discuss the scope and practicalities of your proposed capstone project, ensuring it aligns with the faculty expertise available in the department.

### Question 9: Is it possible that I write a book as my History Capstone?  
### Answer: 

The History Capstone Handbook, AY2025-26 does not explicitly mention writing a book as a possible format for the Capstone project. However, it does state that if students propose a project in an alternative format, the History faculty will confer and establish guidelines for the new format. This suggests that while a book might not be a standard option, it could potentially be considered if the student proposes it and the faculty agrees to the format.

The handbook emphasizes that both thesis and non-thesis Capstones should 'work up' sources that do not figure extensively in existing scholarship, rather than taking the form of a literature review or commentary on existing secondary sources. Writing a book would likely involve working with such primary sources and developing original research, which aligns with the guidelines.

However, it is important to discuss this idea with a potential advisor to ensure that the project meets the expectations and requirements of the Capstone. The advisor can provide guidance on how to structure the project, what the expectations are for original research, and how the book would be evaluated.

In summary, while writing a book is not explicitly mentioned as a format option, it could be considered if proposed and approved by the History faculty, and it would need to align with the Capstone's emphasis on original research and working with primary sources. It is crucial to discuss this with a potential advisor to navigate the process successfully.

Description In masonry, the “capstone” is the stone that sits on top of, or “caps” an arch, wall, or other structure, giving it a polished appearance while demonstrating the strength of the underlying structure. Similarly, the senior Capstone project is intended to “cap off” your undergraduate education with a polished piece of original research that demonstrates your mastery of particular skills and knowledge—in this case, historical reasoning on a subject of your choosing. The Capstone project is a year-long undertaking with less structure than a traditional course. It is certainly not the easiest way to complete your degree, and it may not be the most logical option for you, depending on your interests and your post-graduation plans. You should not consider it to be the default pathway to finishing your degree. One of the best reasons to undertake a Capstone project is if you enjoy doing historical research (which you will have sampled in Hands-On History and the research

The main role of your Capstone advisor is to guide you through the research process and to evaluate your mastery of that process, regardless of the topic. ### Question 10: Given I have finished these subjects: Advanced Deep Learning, Machine Learning, Deep Learning, Database. Are they sufficient for the Advanced Courses requirement of the computer science major?  
### Answer: 

The Advanced Courses requirement for the computer science major likely involves taking advanced courses beyond the introductory level. Based on the provided information, the courses you've completed—Advanced Deep Learning (CS311), Machine Learning (CS307), Deep Learning (CS313), and Database Systems (CS304)—are all advanced courses in their respective areas.

Here's a breakdown of how they fit into the advanced course requirement:

- **Advanced Deep Learning (CS311)**: This course is specifically designed to provide an in-depth understanding of advanced architectures of deep neural networks and algorithms, which is an advanced topic in the field of computer science.

- **Machine Learning (CS307)**: This course covers foundational machine learning concepts and techniques, which is also an advanced topic.

- **Deep Learning for AI (CS313)**: This course is another advanced deep learning course, focusing on practical AI solutions in various domains.

- **Database Systems (CS304)**: While not specifically listed as an "advanced" course, it is an advanced topic in computer science that builds upon the foundational concepts introduced in introductory database courses.

Given these courses, you have covered a broad spectrum of advanced topics within computer science, including machine learning, deep learning, and databases. However, the specific requirements for the "Advanced Courses" may vary depending on the institution and the particular curriculum. It's always a good idea to check with the department or the academic advisor to ensure that the courses you've taken meet the specific requirements for the advanced courses in your major.

In summary, based on the information provided, the courses you've completed are likely sufficient to meet the advanced courses requirement for the computer science major, but it's important to verify this with the relevant academic authorities at your institution.

### Question 11: I have a question if I take a gap year, will my financial aid be canceled? If yes, can I still re-apply for the financial aid program when I get back to school?  
### Answer: 

Students who are taking a gap year and are on a Leave of Absence will not have their financial aid automatically canceled. However, they must re-apply for financial aid when they return to school. The re-application process ensures that the student's financial situation and eligibility for aid are reassessed based on current criteria. It is important for students to meet with the Student Financial Services team before taking a leave to discuss their options and to understand the implications for their financial aid package. The re-application process involves submitting the necessary paperwork and possibly updating the Free Application for Federal Student Aid (FAFSA) or other required forms, depending on the type of aid received. Students should plan to re-apply for financial aid well in advance of their return to ensure a smooth transition and to avoid any delays in receiving aid for the upcoming term. It is crucial to maintain open communication with the financial aid office throughout the leave period and upon return to stay informed about any changes in policies or requirements.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 15]
balance is clear. If the student anticipates being unable to clear the account on time, the student should meet with the Student Financial Services team as soon as possible to request assistance and alternative solutions. After the approved period of absence, a student is put on Administrative Leave of Absence (Admin LOA) for a maximum of one term if the Registrar’s Office receives no response of return confirmation or no registration in the returning term. After one term of Admin LOA, a student is officially withdrawn from Fulbright unless they have contacted the Registrar’s Office and confirmed the plan to return by registering for course(s) or have been granted a leave extension, a maximum of another term. The extension must be endorsed by the Registrar’s Office and approved by the Dean’s Office.

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 11]
student must meet with the Dean’s Office, who establishes the conditions that the student must meet prior to returning to Fulbright. While on suspension, a student may not visit the Fulbright campus unless permission is given from the Dean’s Office or the Vice President of Student Affairs for the purpose of attending relevant meetings. Suspensions typically are for one term or one academic year. A student may be suspended a maximum of two times. A student will not be suspended a third time but will instead have their enrolment in the program canceled. A student in this situation must apply for re-admission. The student must include with his or her application for re-admission a letter that addresses the reasons for their suspensions and cancellation, and the measures they have taken to address those reasons. The re-admission decision is made by the Dean’s Office in consultation with the appropriate members of faculty and staff. Students who are re-admitted must also re- apply for financial aid.

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 15]

The academic record of the student remains unchanged upon reinstatement except under special circumstances decided by the Dean’s Office.

### Question 12: I am aware that Fulbright has to organize online classes instead of on-campus ones. Therefore, will there be any change in the tuition fee?  
### Answer: 

No, there will be no change in the tuition fee. The tuition fee policy at Fulbright remains the same regardless of whether courses are delivered online, on-campus, or in a blended format. Students are still required to pay the same tuition fee for their courses, and the withdrawal and course change policies also apply the same way. The registration process and the approval of course load or maximum course load requests also follow the same procedures. Additionally, students on a Leave of Absence are still considered enrolled in the Fulbright undergraduate program and are subject to the same policies and procedures, including financial obligations. The transition to online classes does not affect the tuition fee structure or the academic policies set forth by Fulbright University.

In the case of approved exceptional circumstances, tuition credit will be considered and follow the “Fees & Charges Guide” for undergraduate students. No reduction in tuition is granted to students whose course withdrawal results in fewer than 12 academic credits. ### Question 13: If I major in Economics, is it sufficient that I take Scholar Development to fulfill Experiential Learning requirements?  
### Answer: 

Experiential Learning can count toward elective or major requirements (upon advisor approval). However, the specific course "Scholar Development" is not mentioned as a part of Fulbright University Vietnam's Experiential Learning Program (ELP) streams, which are housed within Academic Affairs (AA), Career Services (CS), and Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation (CEI). The ELP requires students to complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the program as a graduation requirement. 

The ELP streams offer various experiences such as internships, research projects, community service, and entrepreneurship initiatives, among others. To determine if "Scholar Development" can count toward the Experiential Learning requirement, you would need to consult with your academic advisor. They can provide guidance based on the specific course content and how it aligns with the objectives of the Experiential Learning Program. 

In summary, while "Scholar Development" might offer valuable learning experiences, it is not guaranteed to automatically fulfill the Experiential Learning requirement without advisor approval. It is important to discuss with your advisor to ensure that the course meets the criteria for experiential learning at Fulbright University Vietnam.

### Question 14: Is it sufficient for me to complete 4 credits in EL if I major in Engineering?  
### Answer: 

Yes, it is sufficient to complete 4 credits in EL if you major in Engineering. According to the provided context, all full-time, undergraduate, degree-seeking students must complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the Experiential Learning Program (ELP) as a graduation requirement. The Engineering major requirements specifically mention that Experiential Learning can count toward elective or major requirements (upon advisor approval). Therefore, as long as your advisor approves, completing the minimum 4 credits in EL will satisfy the requirement for your Engineering major. However, it's important to note that the maximum allowed is 12 credits, so you have the flexibility to take more if you wish, up to that limit.

For example, an exploratory course can also be counted as a major course for the corresponding major. However, the number of double-counted credits is often limited (e.g., see subsections 13.2 and 13.3). Additionally, any particular course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements. For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. ### Question 15: Can I take all four engineering foundation courses and only two intermediate engineering courses?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you can take all four Engineering Foundation courses and only two Intermediate Engineering courses. The sequence of courses on each Engineering specialization ranges from Foundation level (i.e., 100-level) through Intermediate level (i.e., 200-level) to Advanced level (i.e., 300-level). This means you can take four courses at the 100-level (Foundation level) and two courses at the 200-level (Intermediate level) before moving on to the Advanced level courses and Capstone projects. However, it's important to note that the specific requirements may vary depending on the particular Engineering specialization you choose, so always check the course catalog or consult with an academic advisor for the most accurate information. Additionally, while you have the freedom to choose elective courses to pursue your interests alongside engineering, you must also complete a well-balanced set of courses in mathematics, natural sciences, arts, humanities, and social sciences to be awarded a bachelor's degree in engineering.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Major Descriptions and Flowcharts_OfficialVersion.pdf, Page: 46]
been exposed to understanding the users' needs, defining engineering problems, and ideating potential solutions before gaining some hands-on experience creating, making, and testing a physical product. Students interested in exploring further can take several Foundation Engineering courses, which are designed specifically to bridge engineering practice, mathematics, and basic sciences. In general, these courses only require the core engineering course as the prerequisite, if any, making them accessible to undetermined-major students to pursue courses of interest. Once the students declare their major or minor in Engineering, they will gain more specialized knowledge and skills by taking more Engineering Foundation courses and Engineering Advanced courses. As part of a human-centered approach that aims to determine human-centric problems and then apply innovative engineering solutions, the students will be encouraged to take Elective courses in Arts, Humanities, Social Sciences, and Entrepreneurship. ### Question 16: If I have completed the course Intro to AI, will CS207 - Object-Oriented Analysis and Design and CS211 – Operating Systems suffice to fill the rest two intermediate courses?  
### Answer: 

CS207 - Object-Oriented Analysis and Design is an intermediate course in Computer Science. It focuses on the principles and practices of object-oriented analysis and design, which are crucial for developing complex software systems. This course would typically build upon the foundational knowledge gained in introductory programming courses, such as CS101 or CS104.

CS211 – Operating Systems, on the other hand, is also an intermediate course that provides an in-depth understanding of operating systems, including their design, implementation, and management of computer resources. This course would also typically require a solid understanding of computer architecture and programming concepts, which are usually covered in introductory courses.

Given that you have completed the Intro to AI course (CS204), which provides a foundational understanding of artificial intelligence, it is likely that these two courses (CS207 and CS211) would be suitable to fulfill the intermediate course requirements for the Computer Science major, assuming they are part of the required curriculum for your specific program.

However, it's important to note that the sufficiency of these courses to meet the requirements for your degree program depends on the specific requirements set by your institution. Some programs may have specific prerequisites or co-requisites for certain courses, and they might also have additional requirements that need to be met.

Therefore, while CS207 and CS211 are likely to be relevant intermediate courses, you should check with your academic advisor or the course catalog to ensure that they meet the specific requirements for your degree program. It's always best to confirm with the official sources to avoid any potential issues with your academic progression.

### Question 17: Does completing both Microeconomics and Macroeconomics satisfy the core theory requirement, or is an additional course needed?  
### Answer: 

Completing both Microeconomics and Macroeconomics satisfies the core theory requirement for declaring an Economics major. According to the provided context, students must complete at least one of Microeconomic Analysis and Macroeconomic Analysis to formally declare the Economics major. Therefore, taking both of these courses would meet this requirement. The context specifies that these courses are part of the foundation requirements for the major, and no additional course is explicitly mentioned as being required beyond these two.

Courses: Principles of Economics 1 and 2 (100-level) These two courses introduce microeconomics and macroeconomics, providing an overview of economic theories and analytical techniques applied to current issues. ### Question 18: If I want to major in Engineering, can I choose other programming courses besides Computer Science I and Computer Science II, for instance Introduction to data visualization?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you can choose other programming courses besides Computer Science I and Computer Science II if you want to major in Engineering. The course "Introduction to Data Visualization" (CS103) is specifically designed to provide students with a set of skills to analyze the nature of data across different domains. It focuses on data visualization, understanding how data are generated, analyzed, and used, and how to apply concepts and skills to visualize data, interpret findings, and examine the impacts of data-driven decisions. This course can be a valuable addition to an Engineering major, especially if data analysis and visualization are relevant to the student's interests and career goals.

The course "Introduction to Data Visualization" (CS103) is listed as a course that aligns with PLO1 (demonstrating an understanding of fundamental computer science concepts) and PLO2 (explaining emerging aspects of the computer science discipline, such as data science). It is designed to complement the foundational courses in Computer Science and can provide students with additional skills that are increasingly important in engineering fields, where data analysis and visualization are becoming more integral.

In the curriculum structure provided, CS103 is listed as a course that can be taken after completing CS101 (Introduction to Programming) and before or concurrently with other intermediate and advanced courses. This indicates that it is a suitable course for students who have a basic understanding of programming and wish to specialize in data science and visualization.

Electives are also mentioned as a way for students to explore different subjects, including those outside the core Computer Science curriculum. Therefore, if "Introduction to Data Visualization" (CS103) aligns with the student's interests and career goals, it can be chosen as an elective to complement the required courses for an Engineering major.

However, it's important to note that the specific requirements for an Engineering major can vary depending on the institution and the particular program. Students should consult with their academic advisor to ensure that any additional courses, including CS103, fit within the requirements of their chosen Engineering major.

In summary, while Computer Science I and Computer Science II are foundational courses for a Computer Science major, students pursuing an Engineering degree can choose from a broader range of courses, including specialized ones like "Introduction to Data Visualization" (CS103), to enhance their skill set and tailor their education to their specific interests and career aspirations. Always check with your academic advisor to ensure that your course selections meet the requirements of your major.

### Question 19: Given I took Discrete Mathematics in Spring 2022 and earned B, then the same course in Spring 2024 and earned C-, which grades will be kept?  
### Answer: 

The higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript.

### Question 20: Can I register 300-level math courses as exploratory courses E4?  
### Answer: 

No, the Exploratory requirements specify that students must take courses at the 100-level or 200-level. The context states that students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses) chosen from each of the three categories (E1, E2, and STEM), and these courses must be at the 100-level or 200-level. Therefore, 300-level math courses cannot be registered as exploratory courses E4. The rules are clear that only courses at the lower levels can fulfill the Exploratory requirements. The notes also mention that students should aim to complete these requirements by the end of their second year, and that the 8-credit limit is independently applied to each major if a student has more than one major. The references to the class of 2023 being exempted and the different requirements for other classes do not affect the current question about 300-level courses.

### Question 21: Can I register for more than 20 credits in a semester?  
### Answer: 

Students who wish to take more than 16 credit hours in one term must submit the Petition for Alternative Course Load or Maximum Course Load Request form to the Registrar's Office. The request form must be approved and signed by the Academic/Major Advisor before submission. So, the answer is Students who wish to take more than 16 credit hours in one term must submit the Petition for Alternative Course Load or Maximum Course Load Request form to the Registrar's Office. The request form must be approved and signed by the Academic/Major Advisor before submission.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
and a minor) share cross- listed or tagged1 courses, up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements. This limit is per each pair of majors (or major and minor) and does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits or courses a student can claim. For example, a student can major in both A and B and minor in C with a total of 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C. This rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged. For instance, if major A requires certain courses from major B for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit. 1 In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements mainly consist of courses from other majors. ### Question 23: For a student doing the literature capstone, if a student includes charts or tables, do they count toward the word limit?  
### Answer: 

The guidelines provided do not explicitly state whether charts or tables count toward the word limit. However, they do specify that the word count includes "endnotes or footnotes" but excludes "full bibliography and images with captions." This suggests that while textual content (words) is the primary focus of the word count, non-textual elements like images and captions are excluded. Charts and tables, being non-textual elements, are likely to be treated similarly to images and captions, meaning they would not count toward the word limit. However, it's crucial for students to clarify this with their academic institution or advisor to avoid any misunderstandings, as specific policies may vary.

### Question 24: If I failed Capstone I in the Fall 2024, can I redo it in Spring 2025?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you can repeat Capstone I in the Spring 2025 term if you failed it in the Fall 2024. According to the university policy, students are required to repeat core courses in the case of receiving the grade F. Since you received an F in Capstone I, you are eligible to repeat the course. However, it's important to consult with your academic/major advisor, the course instructor, and the Registrar's Office to ensure that you are following the correct procedure and that there is space available in the course for the next term. When repeating a course, the higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation, while the lower grade will be overwritten with an "RP" notation, meaning "repeated," and remain on your transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. Remember, repeating a course does not automatically guarantee admission into the next Capstone II, so you should also plan accordingly to meet all other requirements for progression in the capstone sequence.

### Question 25: If I do Literature capstone in Decolonial studies, can it be approved?  
### Answer: 

The Literature capstone project can be approved if there is faculty expertise in the chosen field. In this case, since the faculty expertise includes postcolonial studies, a capstone project in Decolonial studies would likely be approved. However, projects must also be suitable and feasible, and students must consult with prospective advisor(s) early in their junior year to ensure the project aligns with faculty expertise and can be successfully undertaken. It's also important to note that the project must demonstrate originality, comprehensive research, and the ability to connect literary works with social, political, and conceptual nuances, as outlined in the MLO5 (compare historical and transnational literary traditions with attention to social, political and conceptual nuance). Therefore, while faculty expertise is a necessary condition, the project's quality and alignment with the program's learning outcomes are also crucial for approval.

### Question 26: I am a Co24 majoring in Computer Science and taking the Database course. Does it count as major courses?  
### Answer: 

Yes, CS 304: Database Systems is a major course for Computer Science majors. According to the course catalog, CS 304 is listed under the advanced courses section, which requires students to take four advanced courses (16 credits) as part of their major requirements. Therefore, taking CS 304 counts towards fulfilling the advanced course requirement for the Computer Science major.

### Question 27: Can Discrete Mathematics be counted for both Computer Science and Applied Mathematics majors?  
### Answer: 

Yes, Discrete Mathematics (MATH 202) can be counted for both Computer Science and Applied Mathematics majors. In the context provided, Discrete Mathematics is listed as a required course for the Computer Science major (under the "Major Requirements" section) and also as a potential course that Applied Mathematics students might take (under the "Sample Pure mathematics courses" section). This indicates that the course can be applied to the requirements of both majors, fulfilling different roles in each program. For Computer Science, Discrete Mathematics (MATH 202) is one of the foundational courses necessary for the major, along with other Computer Science-specific courses like CS 101, CS 201, CS 203, and CS 302. For Applied Mathematics, while it is not explicitly listed as a required course, it is included in the sample list of Pure Mathematics courses that students might take to fulfill their Pure Mathematics requirement. This flexibility allows students to leverage their coursework effectively, meeting the specific needs of each major while potentially streamlining their academic path by counting a single course towards multiple requirements.

### Question 28: What steps should I take to ensure a smooth transfer of my exchange semester credits from Bard College to Fulbright?  
### Answer: 

To ensure a smooth transfer of your exchange semester credits from Bard College to Fulbright, follow these steps:

1. **Gather Required Information**: Compile all necessary details about the Bard College courses you wish to transfer, including course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments.

2. **Application for Credit Transfer**: Submit an Application for Credit Transfer to the Office of the Registrar at Fulbright University Vietnam. This application should include the required information about the Bard College courses.

3. **Official Transcript**: Ensure that an official transcript with a seal from Bard College is sent directly to the Office of the Registrar at Fulbright University Vietnam. The email address is registrar@fulbright.edu.vn, or you can send it by mail to the address specified in the Transfer Credit Policy.

4. **Check Transfer Credit Policy**: Before submitting your request, read the Transfer Credit Policy V1.0 published in the One-Stop portal to understand the criteria and process.

5. **Declaration**: Complete the Application for Credit Transfer form, including your personal information and a declaration that the information provided is accurate.

6. **Consult with Academic Advisor**: Discuss your program plan with your Academic/Major Advisor at Fulbright to ensure that the transferred credits will meet your degree requirements.

7. **Monitor Grade Release**: Keep an eye on the One Stop portal for the posting of final grades by the Registrar’s Office.

8. **Follow Up**: After submitting your application, follow up with the Office of the Registrar to ensure that your application is being processed and to inquire about the status of your transfer credit evaluation.

Remember, Fulbright will not evaluate transferable credit prior to acceptance, so the transferable credit is determined after you have enrolled at Fulbright. Make sure to adhere to the deadlines and requirements specified in the Transfer Credit Policy to avoid any delays in the transfer process.

Note: The maximum number of transferable credits is 64 hours, so ensure that the credits you are seeking to transfer align with this limit and the requirements for earning a Fulbright University Vietnam undergraduate degree.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 10]
courses delivered by Fulbright. When applying for Credit Transfer, a student is responsible for providing all the information that is requested about the course(s) for which they are applying to transfer. Information may include but not limited to course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments. To apply for Credit Transfer or an Exemption, students should submit an Application for Credit Transfer, along with the required information on the course(s), to the Office of the Registrar. The Registrar will coordinate with the Office of the Dean to determine whether credit or an exemption is justified. Credit Transfer from A-level, Advanced Placement (AP), International Baccalaureate (IB), and Cambridge International Exam (CIE) courses will be determined according to the AP/IB/CIE Credit Policy (in development). For more information, please refer to the Transfer Credit policy_Final_V3.0 published in the One Stop portal.

### Question 29: Are 100-level and 200-level courses that are not explicitly categorized as E1, E2, E3, or E4 also excluded from fulfilling the Exploratory requirement?  
### Answer: 

Yes, according to the given context, only courses specifically chosen from the categories E1 (Arts and Humanities), E2 (Social Sciences), and STEM can be used to fulfill the Exploratory requirements. Courses at the 100-level or 200-level that are not explicitly categorized into these exploratory categories are excluded from fulfilling the Exploratory requirement. The text states that students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses) from each of the three specified categories (E1, E2, and STEM) and no course can be taken to fulfill more than one category. Therefore, courses not fitting into these categories cannot be used to meet the Exploratory requirement.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
and passions lie. Students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses at the 100-level or 200-level) chosen from each of the three categories: E1- Arts and Humanities, E2-Social Sciences, and STEM. No course can be taken to fulfill more than one category. At most, 8 exploratory credits can be taken as Pass/No Pass. Up to two 8 exploratory credits (2 courses) can be counted towards the student’s major requirements. If a student has more than one major, the 8-credit limit is independently applied to each major. ### Question 30: I am a Co25 student pursuing a Computer Science major, can I choose to follow either the old flowchart or the new one?  
### Answer: 

The question does not specify which "old flowchart" or "new one" you are referring to. However, based on the provided context, the university has established a structured plan for Computer Science majors, which includes foundational courses, intermediate courses, and advanced electives. The structure is designed to ensure students gain a comprehensive understanding of computer science fundamentals, develop problem-solving skills, and explore emerging areas within the discipline.

The sample plan provided outlines a typical progression through the major, starting with Introduction to CS and Computer Science I & II, followed by foundational courses in data structures, computer organization, and discrete math. Intermediate courses then delve into algorithms, computer networks, and software engineering. Advanced electives allow students to specialize in areas of interest such as machine learning, database systems, or computer security.

For a Co25 student (presumably a student who entered the university in the 25th cohort), the university would likely recommend following the most current curriculum plan, which is represented by the "new" flowchart. This is to ensure that the student receives the most up-to-date education and is exposed to the latest developments in the field.

However, if there are specific reasons for following the "old" flowchart (e.g., transfer credits, personal preference, or previous curriculum requirements), the student should consult with their academic advisor. The advisor can provide guidance on how to align the "old" flowchart with the current curriculum requirements and ensure that all necessary courses are completed to meet the degree requirements.

In summary, while the university encourages adherence to the new curriculum plan, flexibility may be allowed for students with valid reasons for following an older structure. Always consult with an academic advisor to make an informed decision that aligns with your academic goals and the university's requirements.

### Question 31: If I withdraw from a course after the 4th week of the semester, will I be deducted 4 credits of financial aid?  
### Answer: 

No, withdrawing from a course after the 4th week of the semester will not necessarily result in a deduction of 4 credits of financial aid. According to the provided policy, withdrawing from a course after the halfway point (end of week 4 for an 8-week course or end of week 8 for a full-term course) results in the student receiving an 'F' grade, which will affect the GPA. However, the financial aid eligibility is specifically mentioned to be affected if the withdrawal results in fewer than the minimum credits for full-time enrollment, which is 12 credits. Therefore, if a student withdraws from a course after the 4th week but remains enrolled in at least 12 credits, their financial aid should not be reduced by 4 credits. It is important for students to consult with the financial aid office to understand the specific impact on their financial aid package based on their individual circumstances and remaining course load.

A student who withdraws from a course beyond the halfway point of that course (end of week 4 for an 8-week course or end of week 8 for a full-term course) automatically receives a grade of ‘F’, which will affect the student’s GPA. Exceptions will only be permitted in exceptional circumstances (see Section 12), and it requires supporting documentation as well as the authorization of course instructor(s), the student’s academic/major advisor, and the Registrar’s Office. ### Question 32: Why do I need to take 8 exploratory courses?  
### Answer: 

The Exploratory requirements encourage students to step out of their comfort zone by exploring broad areas of study and discover more fully where their interests and passions lie. Students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses at the 100-level or 200-level) chosen from each of the three categories: E1- Arts and Humanities, E2-Social Sciences, and STEM. This requirement helps students to gain a broader understanding of various fields and potentially discover new areas of interest that may align with their future academic and career paths.

### Question 33: Can I take 160 credits in 4 years given that I pay for the full tuition fee?  
### Answer: 

Fulbright requires students to earn a minimum of 128 credit hours to graduate with an undergraduate degree. The typical full-time course load is 16 credit hours per term or 4 courses of 4 credit hours each. To make standard progress toward completing the degree, students are required to register for no fewer than 12 credit hours and no more than 20 credit hours per term. Therefore, it is possible to take up to 160 credits in 4 years by registering for the maximum allowed credits each term, which is 20 credit hours. However, students must ensure they meet all academic requirements, including those for exploratory courses and major requirements, and they must also consider the impact on their financial aid eligibility and tuition costs.

The references provided are relevant to the question as they outline the credit hour requirements, the typical course load, and the maximum course load allowed per term. The information provided covers all necessary aspects to determine whether it is possible to take 160 credits in 4 years, including the implications for tuition fees and financial aid.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 7]
of credit hours a student successfully completes with a grade of D or higher for letter grade options and a Pass for Pass/No Pass options. Attempted credits are the credit hours students enroll in and are recorded in the transcript, regardless of whether they complete the courses or pass them. This includes all courses for which a student receives a grade, including withdrawals (W), incompletes (I), repeats (RP), and failures (F). See section 4 for more details on different types of grades. To graduate with an undergraduate degree from Fulbright, a student must earn a minimum of 128 credit hours.  The typical full-time course load is 16 credit hours per term or 4 courses of 4 credit hours each. In order to make standard progress toward completing the degree, students are required to register for no fewer than 12 credit hours and no more than 20 credit hours per term. All students for whom English is not their first language must take an English Assessment Test required by NECHE Accreditation. 13.8  Double Counting In satisfying degree requirements, some courses can be double-counted. For example, an exploratory course can also be counted as a major course for the corresponding major. However, the number of double-counted credits is often limited (e.g., see subsections 13.2 and 13.3). Additionally, any particular course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements. For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. Triple counting is not allowed. 13.9  Maximal Length of Study Fulbright follows MOET regulations, which state that the maximum time to complete the program cannot exceed double the standard

### Question 35: How is the grade of a replaced course displayed on the transcript? Will it replace the old one or create a new entry in the transcript?  
### Answer: 

The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 10]
advisor, the course instructor, and the Registrar’s Office. Considerations will include course availability and capacity in the next academic term or next academic year. A higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. A student may not retake the same course for the third time unless he/she failed the second time or if a specific grade is needed to meet the major requirements. Students shall consult with their Academic/Major Advisor about their program plan if they are considering taking the class for a third time. 4.2 Final Grade Release Final grades will be posted by the Registrar’s Office and accessible via the One Stop portal. 4.3 Credit Transfer and Exemptions earned from other universities. A student who has earned credit at another school or university may apply to have that credit counted towards their

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 10]
• The Registrar's Office informs the Dean’s Office of the case. After investigation, if the Deans can mediate, no further action is required. Otherwise, the Registrar's Office organizes a panel review with the Dean’s Office for an independent evaluation of the grade. The grade determined after this evaluation shall be the final grade. In cases concerning the interpretation of a course policy, the Dean’s Office will defer to the course syllabus whenever possible and otherwise will make a final ruling on the matter.

Considerations will include course availability and capacity in the next academic term or next academic year. A higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. ### Question 36: What are the courses that can replace the capstone for a given major?  
### Answer: 

According to the given context, a capstone in Computer Science is an individual project that can only take the form of a written thesis. It is designed to demonstrate the principal learning outcomes and competencies of the curriculum in a meaningful and coherent way. The capstone should demonstrate original critical and creative thinking, and primary research is encouraged. The student is expected to carefully consider the scope of their research and production for the capstone project. It is important for the student to consult early on with their potential advisor to ensure a good match. Applications that do not demonstrate an understanding of the scope and practicalities needed to realize the capstone may not be approved.

The learning outcomes for completing the capstone include demonstrating a strong mastery of the major, applying knowledge to new situations, and effectively communicating complex ideas. The capstone objectives are to encourage original critical and creative thinking, engage in primary research, and produce a coherent and meaningful project that reflects the student's learning and skills.

In terms of replacing the capstone, the context does not explicitly mention alternative courses. The capstone is described as a mandatory, individual project that must be a written thesis in the Computer Science major. There is no mention of courses that can replace this requirement. Therefore, based on the provided information, the capstone cannot be replaced by any other courses within the major.

Relevant to the question, the capstone is described as a mandatory individual project in the form of a written thesis for the Computer Science major. It is designed to demonstrate the student's mastery of the major and cannot be replaced by other courses according to the given context. The context does not provide information about alternative courses that could replace the capstone requirement.

The references provided do not offer any additional information on this topic beyond the description of the capstone requirement in the Computer Science major. Therefore, based on the given information, the capstone cannot be replaced by any other courses within the major. The capstone is a unique, required component of the curriculum that must be completed as a written thesis project.

In summary, the capstone in Computer Science is a mandatory, individual project in the form of a written thesis. It cannot be replaced by any other courses according to the provided context. The learning outcomes and objectives of the capstone are to demonstrate mastery of the major, encourage original thinking, and engage in primary research. There is no mention of alternative courses that could serve as a replacement for the capstone requirement in the Computer Science major.

### Question 37: Is it possible to graduate with double majors in Human-Centered Engineering and Applied Mathematics? If yes, please provide a sample study plan.  
### Answer: 

It is possible to graduate with double majors in Human-Centered Engineering and Applied Mathematics, but it requires careful planning and adherence to the university's course requirements for both majors. Here is a sample study plan:

**Year 1:**
- **Fall Semester:**
  - General Education Core Courses (e.g., English, Natural Sciences) (12 credits)
  - Engineering Exploration: Sink or Swim (4 credits)
  - Calculus I (4 credits)
- **Spring Semester:**
  - General Education Exploratory Courses (e.g., Arts, Humanities) (8 credits)
  - Calculus II (4 credits)
  - Introduction to Statistics (4 credits)

**Year 2:**
- **Fall Semester:**
  - Linear Algebra (4 credits)
  - Engineering Foundation Course (e.g., Dynamics, Materials Science) (4 credits)
  - General Education Core Course (4 credits)
- **Spring Semester:**
  - Multivariable Calculus (4 credits)
  - Human-Centered Engineering Course (e.g., Design Thinking) (4 credits)
  - General Education Exploratory Course (4 credits)

**Year 3:**
- **Fall Semester:**
  - Ideas in Mathematics/Advanced Mathematical Writing (4 credits)
  - Intermediate Applied Mathematics Course (e.g., Differential Equations) (4 credits)
  - Human-Centered Engineering Course (4 credits)
- **Spring Semester:**
  - Intermediate Human-Centered Engineering Course (4 credits)
  - Pure Mathematics Course (e.g., Abstract Algebra) (4 credits)
  - Experiential Learning (4 credits)

**Year 4:**
- **Fall Semester:**
  - Advanced Applied Mathematics Course (e.g., Operations Research) (4 credits)
  - Advanced Human-Centered Engineering Course (e.g., Human-Computer Interaction) (4 credits)
  - Research Seminar (4 credits)
- **Spring Semester:**
  - Elective Applied Mathematics Course (4 credits)
  - Elective Human-Centered Engineering Course (4 credits)
  - Capstone I (4 credits)

**Year 5:**
- **Fall Semester:**
  - Advanced Applied Mathematics Course (e.g., Quantitative Finance) (4 credits)
  - Elective Human-Centered Engineering Course (4 credits)
- **Spring Semester:**
  - Capstone II (4 credits)

**Notes:**
- Ensure that all General Education requirements are met.
- Double-counting is not allowed, so courses like Operations Research can only count for either the Applied Mathematics major or as an elective, not both.
- Students must consult with academic advisors in both departments to ensure all requirements are met and to adjust the plan as needed.
- The plan assumes a 5-year program to accommodate the additional coursework required for two majors.

This plan is a guideline and may need adjustments based on course availability, prerequisites, and individual student needs. It is crucial to stay in close communication with academic advisors in both the Human-Centered Engineering and Applied Mathematics departments to ensure all requirements are met and to make any necessary adjustments to the plan.

### Question 38: Are the 4-credit EL counted towards the credits covered by financial aid?  
### Answer: 

All full-time, undergraduate, degree-seeking students must complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the ELP as a graduation requirement. The Experiential Learning Program (ELP) credits are counted toward the graduation requirement, but it is not explicitly stated whether these credits are covered by financial aid. Typically, financial aid is based on the number of credits a student is enrolled in, and students should check with the Financial Aid Office at Fulbright to understand how ELP credits specifically affect their financial aid package. Students should be aware that course withdrawals may affect their financial aid eligibility if resulting in fewer than the minimum credits for full-time enrollment, which is 12 credits. Therefore, while ELP credits are required for graduation, their impact on financial aid eligibility should be verified with the Financial Aid Office.

For instance, if course X is cross-listed between major A and major B and tagged by minor C, then X can be used to fulfill the requirements of major A and major B, or major A and minor C, or major B and minor C. Triple counting is not allowed. 13.9  Maximal Length of Study Fulbright follows MOET regulations, which state that the maximum time to complete the program cannot exceed double the standard

### Question 39: What are the exchange policies of Fulbright?  
### Answer: 

Fulbright University Vietnam (Fulbright) has specific policies and procedures in place to support students in making effective choices as participants in its undergraduate program. These policies include Credit Transfer and Exemption processes. For Credit Transfer, students are responsible for providing all requested information about the course(s) they wish to transfer, which may include course curriculum, learning outcomes, content, and assessments. To apply, students submit an Application for Credit Transfer along with the required information to the Office of the Registrar. The Registrar will coordinate with the Office of the Dean to determine if credit or an exemption is justified. Credit Transfer from A-level, Advanced Placement (AP), International Baccalaureate (IB), and Cambridge International Exam (CIE) courses will be determined according to the AP/IB/CIE Credit Policy (in development). For more detailed information, students should refer to the Transfer Credit policy_Final_V3.0 published in the One Stop portal.

Regarding grading, Fulbright uses a standard letter grade system ranging from 'A' to 'F' with corresponding grade points. The grade point scale is as follows:

Letter Grade Grade Point Grade Category Percentage
A 4.0 Excellent 90-100
A- 3.7 Good 85-89
B+ 3.3 Above Average 80-84
B 3.0 Average 75-79
B- 2.7 Below Average 70-74
C+ 2.3 Poor 65-69
C 2.0 Poor 60-64
D 1.0 Very Poor 50-59
F 0.0 Fail Below 50

To earn a Fulbright University Vietnam undergraduate degree, a student must earn at least 64 credit hours from courses delivered by Fulbright. A maximum of 64 credit hours can be transferred and credited to a Fulbright undergraduate degree.

Electives are available for students to explore different subjects across the curriculum, build a diverse background, or pursue specialized studies within their major. The total number of earned credits must be at least 128 to complete the degree.

A capstone pathway is also offered by all majors for students to complete during their final year, providing an opportunity for in-depth study and application of their major's knowledge and skills.

The Experiential Learning Program (ELP) is another component of Fulbright's undergraduate program, designed to integrate real-world experiences into students' academic journeys. Each team within the ELP is responsible for administrative and academic duties of their respective streams and operates within Fulbright's standard operating procedures, developed in consultation with undergraduate program leadership and faculty.

These policies and procedures reflect Fulbright's commitment to innovation, continuous improvement, and a uniquely student-centered learning environment. The university regularly updates these documents to reflect its growth and evolving educational philosophy.

The references provided (One Stop portal, Transfer Credit policy_Final_V3.0, and the Experiential Learning Program link) are relevant to understanding the specific details and processes related to the exchange policies at Fulbright University Vietnam. However, the core policies and procedures are summarized above and do not require reference to external documents for a basic understanding of Fulbright's exchange policies.

### Question 40: What actions are considered plagiarism? Are AI-generated works counted?  
### Answer: 

Plagiarism is considered a serious violation of academic integrity and is strictly prohibited at Fulbright University Vietnam. According to the Code of Academic Integrity, plagiarism involves presenting someone else's work, ideas, or words as one's own without proper acknowledgment. This includes but is not limited to:

1. Direct copying of text, images, or data without proper citation.
2. Paraphrasing another's work without proper attribution.
3. Using another person's research, data, or ideas without giving them credit.

Regarding AI-generated works, Fulbright University Vietnam has specific regulations in place. Creative projects that incorporate generative AI must include comprehensive documentation covering every aspect of AI usage. This documentation should clearly define the reasons for utilizing generative AI, specify the tools and platforms employed, outline the process and methodology of AI incorporation, and address ethical and copyright issues associated with AI usage.

AI-generated works are not automatically exempt from plagiarism concerns. The key is transparency and proper documentation. If a student uses generative AI to assist in their Capstone project, they must fully document the AI's role in the project. This includes detailing how the AI was used, what aspects of the project were generated or altered by AI, and how ethical and copyright issues were addressed.

In summary, while AI-generated content can be part of a Capstone project, it is crucial to adhere to the academic integrity guidelines, which include thorough documentation of AI usage. Failure to do so could be interpreted as plagiarism, leading to disciplinary actions. Therefore, AI-generated works are not considered exempt from plagiarism concerns; they must be properly documented and attributed to ensure academic integrity.

### Question 41: Why is the graduation requirement 128 credits, but the major requirement is only 48 credits?  
### Answer: 

The graduation requirement of 128 credits is a total requirement that includes all courses a student must take to complete their degree, including general education courses, electives, and major-specific requirements. The major requirement of 48 credits specifically refers to the number of courses or credits that must be completed within a particular major field of study. The major requirement is a subset of the overall graduation requirement.

The 128-credit graduation requirement encompasses a broad range of courses designed to provide a well-rounded education, including:

- Core curriculum or general education courses, which are typically required of all students and cover a variety of disciplines such as humanities, social sciences, natural sciences, and mathematics.
- Major-specific courses, which are the 48 credits required for the chosen major.
- Elective courses, which allow students to explore additional areas of interest beyond their major.
- Experiential learning requirements, which may include internships, research projects, or other hands-on experiences.
- Physical education courses, which are sometimes required to promote physical well-being.

The major requirement of 48 credits is focused solely on the courses necessary to achieve proficiency in the student's chosen field of study. This ensures that students have a strong foundation in their area of specialization while also meeting the broader educational goals set by the institution.

The distinction between the overall graduation requirement and the major requirement allows for flexibility in the curriculum, enabling students to tailor their educational experience to their interests and career goals while still meeting the university's educational standards. The 128-credit requirement ensures that students have a comprehensive educational experience, covering a wide range of subjects and skills, while the 48-credit major requirement ensures that students have a deep understanding of their chosen field.

In summary, the graduation requirement of 128 credits is a comprehensive total that includes all aspects of the undergraduate education, while the major requirement of 48 credits is a specific subset that focuses on the student's chosen area of study. The distinction allows for a balanced educational experience that combines breadth and depth.

### Question 42: Explain the cross-listed mechanism between courses.  
### Answer: 

If two majors (or two minors, or a major and a minor) share cross-listed or tagged courses, up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements. This means that a student can take courses that are listed under more than one major or minor and count those credits toward both their major and minor requirements. For example, a student can major in both A and B and minor in C with a total of 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C. This rule allows students to efficiently use their course credits and potentially reduce the number of courses they need to take to fulfill all their academic requirements. However, this rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged, meaning courses that are specific to one major and not shared with another cannot be double-counted under this mechanism. Courses that are not cross-listed or tagged, such as those required for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, must be taken for the specific major's requirements and cannot be used to satisfy the requirements of another major or minor under this cross-listing rule. Additionally, courses used for double-counting are limited to 8 credits per pair of majors or majors and minors, but this does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits or courses a student can claim overall. In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements often consist of courses from other majors, which are usually not cross-listed with the minors and are referred to as 'tagged' by these minors. These tagged courses also fall under the double-counting rule, allowing students to apply up to 8 credits toward both the minor and another major's requirements if applicable.  

<details>

### Question 44: How does the core quest course selection work to fulfill core credits?  
### Answer: 

The core QUEST course selection involves choosing one course from the group of four courses: Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age, Scientific Inquiry, Introduction to Statistics, and Programming for Data Science and Visualization. Students must complete one of these courses as part of their five core courses requirement, which totals 20 credits. These core courses are pre-assigned to students in their first year or the first semester of the second year. The specific course chosen can vary based on the student's interests and the requirements of their major, but the key point is that one of these courses must be completed to satisfy the core curriculum's quantitative reasoning and scientific inquiry components. The course chosen can be taken for a letter grade or, if applicable, as Pass/No Pass, but it is a mandatory requirement for all students in the classes of 2027 and earlier.

### Question 45: Can I take a 300-level course to fulfill a 200-level major requirement?  
### Answer: 

No, according to the provided context, students must complete the required courses in the correct sequence. Advanced level courses (300-level) in Economics can only be taken after completing at least one of the Microeconomic and Macroeconomic Analysis and Econometrics courses, which are typically 200-level courses. Therefore, a 300-level course cannot be used to fulfill a 200-level major requirement. The requirements for declaring the Economics major specify that students must complete the foundation courses (which are 200-level) before moving on to the advanced courses (300-level). Thus, the sequence of course completion is strictly adhered to, and a 300-level course cannot retroactively satisfy a 200-level requirement.

### Question 46: If I take a course at another university and want to transfer the credits to Fulbright, but Fulbright doesn’t have that major code, yet the course is related to a major at Fulbright, can I count it toward that major?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you can apply for Credit Transfer to have the credits from the course taken at another university count towards your Fulbright undergraduate degree. When applying for Credit Transfer, you are responsible for providing all the necessary information about the course, such as course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments. You should submit an Application for Credit Transfer along with the required information to the Office of the Registrar.

To determine whether credit is justified, the Registrar will coordinate with the Office of the Dean. If the course is evaluated to be equivalent to a course at Fulbright and is related to your major, you may be given an Exemption from being required to take the equivalent course at Fulbright.

Keep in mind that a maximum of 64 credit hours can be transferred and credited to a Fulbright undergraduate degree. This means that to earn a Fulbright University Vietnam undergraduate degree, you must earn at least 64 credit hours from courses delivered by Fulbright.

Additionally, when considering courses for Credit Transfer, remember the rules about cross-listed and tagged courses (as mentioned in sections 13.2, 13.3, and 13.4). A course can only be used to satisfy at most two requirements, and triple counting is not allowed. This means that while the course might be related to your major, it can only contribute to fulfilling a maximum of two different requirements (e.g., major and minor requirements).

Lastly, it's important to note that the maximum time to complete your studies at Fulbright is regulated by MOET and cannot exceed double the standard duration, which is 8 years for an undergraduate program (as mentioned in section 13.9).

These courses are usually not cross-listed with the minors and are referred to as 'tagged' by these minors. 13.4  Experiential Learning Requirements Experiential Learning can count toward elective or major requirements (upon advisor approval).
